- The top number is 7.
- The left number is 1.
- The right number is 3.
- The bottom left number is x.
- The bottom middle number is y.
- The bottom right number is z.

The sums of the numbers on each side of the triangle are equal. Therefore, we can set up the following equations based on the sides of the triangle:

1. The sum of the top number and the left number is equal to the sum of the top number and the right number:
   \[
   7 + 1 = 7 + 3
   \]
   This equation is not useful for finding x and y, so we need to use the other sides.

2. The sum of the top number and the left number is also equal to the sum of the bottom left number and the bottom middle number:
   \[
   7 + 1 = x + y
   \]
   Simplifying, we get:
   \[
   8 = x + y
   \]

3. The sum of the top number and the right number is equal to the sum of the bottom middle number and the bottom right number:
   \[
   7 + 3 = y + z
   \]
   Simplifying, we get:
   \[
   10 = y + z
   \]

Now we have two equations:
1. \( x + y = 8 \)
2. \( y + z = 10 \)

We need to find the sum of the two stained numbers, which are x and z. To do this, we can add the two equations:
\[
(x + y) + (y + z) = 8 + 10
\]
\[
x + 2y + z = 18
\]

We already know that \( x + y = 8 \), so we can substitute \( x + y \) with 8:
\[
8 + y + z = 18
\]
\[
y + z = 10
\]

Now, we need to find the sum of x and z. We can use the equation \( y + z = 10 \) and the fact that \( x + y = 8 \). Since \( y + z = 10 \), we can substitute \( y \) from the first equation:
\[
x + (10 - z) = 8
\]
\[
x + 10 - z = 8
\]
\[
x - z = -2
\]

We need to find \( x + z \). We can add the two equations:
\[
x + y + y + z = 8 + 10
\]
\[
x + 2y + z = 18
\]

Since \( y + z = 10 \), we can substitute \( y + z \) with 10:
\[
x + 10 = 18
\]
\[
x = 8
\]

Now, we can find \( z \):
\[
y + z = 10
\]
\[
8 + z = 10
\]
\[
z = 2
\]

The sum of the two stained numbers is:
\[
x + z = 8 + 2 = 10
\]

The sum of the two numbers is:
\[

Response: <think>
To solve this problem, let's analyze the image step by step:

1. On the left side of the seesaw, there are 3 apples and 1 plum.
2. On the right side of the seesaw, there are 10 plums and 1 apple.

The seesaw is balanced, which means the total weight on both sides is equal. Therefore, the weight of 3 apples plus 1 plum is equal to the weight of 10 plums plus 1 apple.

We can set up the equation to represent this balance:
\[ 3 \text{ apples} + 1 \text{ plum} = 10 \text{ plums} + 1 \text{ apple} \]

To find out how many plums weigh as much as one apple, we need to isolate the variables. Let's denote the weight of one apple as \( A \) and the weight of one plum as \( P \).

From the equation:
\[ 3A + P = 10P + A \]

Rearrange the equation to solve for \( P \) in terms of \( A \):
\[ 3A + P - A = 10P \]
\[ 2A = 9P \]
\[ P = \frac{2A}{9} \]

This equation tells us that one apple weighs the same as 9 plums. Therefore, 9 plums weigh as much as one apple.

</think>
<answer>
9 plums weigh as much as one apple.
</answer>

We are asked to find the number that could be on the opposite side of the face with the number 8. Let's denote the number on the face opposite to 8 as \(x\). Therefore, we have:
\[8 + x = S\]

To find \(x\), we need to determine the value of \(S\). Since the numbers on the faces are chosen from 1 to 9, the sum of all the numbers on the faces of the cube is:
\[1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 + 6 + 7 + 8 + 9 = 45\]

Since there are three pairs of opposite faces, the sum of the numbers on all the faces is:
\[3S = 45\]
\[S = 15\]

Now, we can find \(x\) by substituting \(S = 15\) into the equation:
\[8 + x = 15\]
\[x = 7\]

Therefore, the number that could have been written on the opposite side with the number 8 is 7.
</think>
<answer>7</answer>

3. **Regular Pentagon (5 sides):**
   - The circumcircle of the pentagon divides the plane into 5 regions (inside the pentagon, outside the pentagon, and the pentagon itself).

4. **Regular Hexagon (6 sides):**
   - The circumcircle of the hexagon divides the plane into 6 regions (inside the hexagon, outside the hexagon, and the hexagon itself).

- 3 (from the triangle)
- 4 (from the square)
- 5 (from the pentagon)
- 6 (from the hexagon)
- ...

3. **Use the properties of the quarter circle and the inscribed circle**: The center of circle \( K \) will be at a distance equal to its radius from the center of the quarter circle. Let's denote the radius of circle \( K \) as \( r \).

   - Since there are two smaller semi-circles, the total area of the two smaller semi-circles is \(2 \times 2\pi = 4\pi \, \text{cm}^2\).

3. **Calculate the area of the shaded region:**
   - The area of the shaded region is the area of the rectangle minus the area of the two smaller semi-circles.
   - The area of the shaded region is \(16 - 4\pi \, \text{cm}^2\).

The final answer is \(\boxed{16 - 4\pi}\).

   - Since \( \angle ABC = 90^\circ \), triangle \( ABC \) is a right triangle with \( AB \) as one of the legs.
   - Since \( \angle ACD = 90^\circ \), triangle \( ACD \) is also a right triangle with \( AC \) as one of the legs.

3. **Use trigonometric relationships:**
   - In triangle \( ABC \), \( \angle CAB = \theta \). Therefore, \( \angle BAC = \theta \).
   - In triangle \( ACD \), \( \angle DAC = \theta \). Therefore, \( \angle CAD = \theta \).

4. **Determine the length of \( AC \):**
   - In triangle \( ABC \), \( \angle ABC = 90^\circ \) and \( \angle CAB = \theta \). Using the sine function:
     \[
     \sin(\theta) = \frac{BC}{AB} = \frac{BC}{1} = BC
     \]
     \[
     BC = \sin(\theta)
     \]

5. **Determine the length of \( AC \):**
   - Using the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \( ABC \):
     \[
     AC = \sqrt{AB^2 + BC^2} = \sqrt{1^2 + (\sin(\theta))^2} = \sqrt{1 + \sin^2(\theta)}
     \]

6. **Determine the length of \( AD \):**
   - In triangle \( ACD \), \( \angle ACD = 90^\circ \) and \( \angle DAC = \theta \). Using the sine function:
     \[
     \sin(\theta) = \frac{CD}{AC} = \frac{CD}{\sqrt{1 + \sin^2(\theta)}}
     \]
     \[
     CD = \sin(\theta) \cdot \sqrt{1 + \sin^2(\theta)}
     \]

7. **Use the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \( ACD \):**
   - \( AD \) is the hypotenuse:
     \[
     AD = \sqrt{AC^2 + CD^2} = \sqrt{(\sqrt{1 + \sin^2(\theta)})^2 + (\sin(\theta) \cdot \sqrt{1 + \sin^2(\theta)})^2}
     \]
     \[
     AD = \sqrt{1 + \sin^2(\theta) + \sin^2(\theta) \cdot (1 + \sin^2(\theta))}
     \]
     \[
     AD = \sqrt{1 + \sin^2(\theta) + \sin^2(\theta) + \sin^4(\theta)}
     \]
     \[
     AD = \sqrt{1 + 2\sin^2(\theta) + \sin^4(\theta)}
     \]
     \[
     AD = \sqrt{(1 + \sin^2(\theta))^2}
     \]
     \[
     AD = 1 + \sin^2(\theta)

     \[
     f(-1) = 0 \quad \text{and} \quad f(1) = 0
     \]

   - For \( x = -1 \):
     \[
     f(-1) = a(-1)^3 + b(-1)^2 + c(-1) + d = -a + b - c + d = 0
     \]
   - For \( x = 1 \):
     \[
     f(1) = a(1)^3 + b(1)^2 + c(1) + d = a + b + c + d = 0

     - The number of ways to choose 3 collinear points out of 3 is \( \binom{3}{3} = 1 \).
     - There is only 1 such diagonal, so the total number of collinear sets is 1.

Adding all these together, we get:
\[ 12 \text{ (horizontal)} + 12 \text{ (vertical)} + 4 \text{ (top-left to bottom-right)} + 1 \text{ (top-right to bottom-left)} = 29 \]

</think>
<answer>
29
</answer>

\[ 47 + 63 = 110 \]

\[ a + b = 110 \]

\[ a + 47 = 110 \]

\[ a = 110 - 47 \]
\[ a = 63 \]

\[ 63 + 63 = 126 \]

\[ b + 63 = 110 \]
\[ b = 110 - 63 \]
\[ b = 47 \]

\[ a + b = 63 + 47 = 110 \]

This matches the sum of 110. Therefore, the missing number in field \( a \) is:
\[ a = 63 \]

   - Since N is on the diagonal KM, the distances from N to the sides LM and KL are the legs of a right triangle with the diagonal KM as the hypotenuse.
   - Let the length of LM be \( x \). The distance from N to LM is 1, and the distance from N to KL is 8. Therefore, the length of the segment from N to the point where the perpendicular from N to KL meets KL is 8.

4. **Set up the equation:**
   - The length of the segment from N to the point where the perpendicular from N to KL meets KL is 8, and the length of the segment from N to the point where the perpendicular from N to LM meets LM is 1.
   - The total length of the diagonal KM can be split into two segments: one along LM and one along KL. The segment along LM is 1, and the segment along KL is 8.

5. **Calculate the length of LM:**
   - Since the total length of the diagonal KM is the sum of the segments along LM and KL, we can use the Pythagorean theorem in the context of the rectangle.
   - The length of LM is the sum of the segments along LM and KL, which is \( 1 + 8 = 9 \).

1. **Understanding the Absolute Value Function**:
   - For \(x \geq 0\), \(|x| = x\), so \(x - |x| = 0\).
   - For \(x < 0\), \(|x| = -x\), so \(x - |x| = x - (-x) = 2x\).

   Similarly, for \(y \geq 0\), \(|y| = y\), so \(y - |y| = 0\).
   - For \(y < 0\), \(|y| = -y\), so \(y - |y| = y - (-y) = 2y\).

2. **Simplifying the Equation**:
   - When \(x \geq 0\) and \(y \geq 0\), the equation becomes \(0^2 + 0^2 = 4\), which is not possible.
   - When \(x \geq 0\) and \(y < 0\), the equation becomes \(0^2 + (2y)^2 = 4\), which simplifies to \(4y^2 = 4\) or \(y^2 = 1\). So, \(y = -1\) (since \(y < 0\)).
   - When \(x < 0\) and \(y \geq 0\), the equation becomes \((2x)^2 + 0^2 = 4\), which simplifies to \(4x^2 = 4\) or \(x^2 = 1\). So, \(x = -1\) (since \(x < 0\)).
   - When \(x < 0\) and \(y < 0\), the equation becomes \((2x)^2 + (2y)^2 = 4\), which simplifies to \(4x^2 + 4y^2 = 4\) or \(x^2 + y^2 = 1\).

3. **Graphing the Solutions**:
   - The solutions for \(x \geq 0\) and \(y < 0\) form a vertical line at \(x = -1\).
   - The solutions for \(x < 0\) and \(y \geq 0\) form a vertical line at \(x = -1\).
   - The solutions for \(x < 0\) and \(y < 0\) form a circle with radius 1 centered at \((-1, -1)\).

\[4 + x + 1 = 5 + x\]

   - \( c = 17 \) (hypotenuse)

   The area \( A \) of a right-angled triangle can be calculated using the formula:
   \[
   A = \frac{1}{2} \times a \times b
   \]
   Substituting the given values:
   \[
   A = \frac{1}{2} \times 8 \times 15 = 60
   \]

3. **Calculate the semi-perimeter \( s \) of the triangle:**
   The semi-perimeter \( s \) is given by:
   \[

   \]

4. **Use the formula for the radius \( r \) of the inscribed semicircle:**

To determine which statement is true, we need to calculate the volumes of the ellipsoids formed by rotating the given ellipse about the x-axis and the y-axis.

1. **Volume of the ellipsoid formed by rotating about the x-axis:**
   - The equation of the ellipse is \(\frac{x^2}{b^2} + \frac{y^2}{a^2} = 1\).
   - When rotated about the x-axis, the volume \(V_x\) of the ellipsoid is given by the formula for the volume of a solid of revolution:
     \[
     V_x = \pi \int_{-b}^{b} y^2 \, dx
     \]
   - Substituting \(y^2 = a^2 \left(1 - \frac{x^2}{b^2}\right)\) into the integral:
     \[
     V_x = \pi \int_{-b}^{b} a^2 \left(1 - \frac{x^2}{b^2}\right) \, dx
     \]
   - Simplifying the integral:
     \[
     V_x = \pi a^2 \int_{-b}^{b} \left(1 - \frac{x^2}{b^2}\right) \, dx
     \]
   - Evaluating the integral:
     \[
     V_x = \pi a^2 \left[ x - \frac{x^3}{3b^2} \right]_{-b}^{b}
     \]
     \[
     V_x = \pi a^2 \left( \left( b - \frac{b^3}{3b^2} \right) - \left( -b + \frac{(-b)^3}{3b^2} \right) \right)
     \]
     \[
     V_x = \pi a^2 \left( b - \frac{b}{3} + b + \frac{b}{3} \right)
     \]
     \[
     V_x = \pi a^2 \left( 2b \right)
     \]
     \[
     V_x = 2\pi a^2 b
     \]

2. **Volume of the ellipsoid formed by rotating about the y-axis:**
   - The equation of the ellipse is \(\frac{x^2}{b^2} + \frac{y^2}{a^2} = 1\).
   - When rotated about the y-axis, the volume \(V_y\) of the ellipsoid is given by the formula for the volume of a solid of revolution:
     \[
     V_y = \pi \int_{-a}^{a} x^2 \, dy
     \]
   - Substituting \(x^2 = b^2 \left(1 - \frac{y^2}{a^2}\right)\) into the integral:
     \[
     V_y = \pi \int_{-a}^{a} b^2 \left(1 - \frac{y^2}{a^2}\right) \, dy
     \]
   - Simplifying the integral:
     \[
     V_y = \pi b^2 \int_{-a}^{a} \left(1 - \frac{y^2}{a^2}\right) \, dy
     \]
   - Evaluating the integral:
     \[
     V_y = \pi b^2 \left[ y - \frac{y^3}{3a^2} \right]_{-a}^{a}
     \]
     \[
     V_y = \pi b^2 \left( \left( a - \frac{a^3}{3a^2} \right) - \left( -a + \frac{(-a)^3}{3a^2} \right) \right)
     \]
     \[
     V_y = \pi b^2 \left( a - \frac{a}{3} + a + \frac{a}{3} \right)
     \]
     \[
     V_y = \pi b^2 \left( 2a \right)
     \]
     \[
     V_y = 2\pi a^2 b
     \]

Since both volumes are equal, the statement that is true is that the volumes of the ellipsoids formed by rotating the ellipse about the x-axis and the y-axis are the same.

The point on the triangle will trace out a path that is equivalent to the perimeter of a circle with a radius equal to the distance from the center of the triangle to the point on the triangle.

1. **Identify the radius of the path**: The radius of the path is the distance from the center of the equilateral triangle to one of its vertices. For an equilateral triangle with side length \( s \), the distance from the center to a vertex (the circumradius \( R \)) can be calculated using the formula:
   \[
   R = \frac{s}{\sqrt{3}}
   \]
   Since the side length \( s \) of the equilateral triangle is equal to the side length of the unit square (which is 1), the circumradius \( R \) is:
   \[
   R = \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}
   \]

2. **Determine the path length**: The point on the triangle will trace out a path that is equivalent to the circumference of a circle with radius \( R \). The circumference \( C \) of a circle is given by:
   \[
   C = 2\pi R
   \]
   Substituting the value of \( R \):
   \[
   C = 2\pi \left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}\right) = \frac{2\pi}{\sqrt{3}} = \frac{2\pi \sqrt{3}}{3}
   \]

Therefore, the path length that the point covers is:
\[
\boxed{\frac{2\pi \sqrt{3}}{3}}
\]

To find the radius of the circle that touches all the inscribed diagonals of a regular octagon with side length 10, we need to understand the geometric properties of the octagon and the circle.

1. **Identify the type of octagon**: The octagon is regular, meaning all its sides and angles are equal.
2. **Understand the diagonals**: In a regular octagon, the diagonals that touch the circle are the ones that connect opposite vertices (the longest diagonals).
3. **Calculate the length of the longest diagonal**: The longest diagonal of a regular octagon is equal to the side length multiplied by the golden ratio (approximately 1.618) for a square, but for a regular octagon, it's more complex. However, for a regular octagon, the longest diagonal is approximately equal to the side length times the square root of 2 plus 2 (which is approximately 4.242 times the side length).
4. **Determine the radius of the inscribed circle**: The radius of the circle that touches all the inscribed diagonals is the same as the radius of the circle that touches the sides of the octagon, which is the inradius of the octagon.

The inradius \( r \) of a regular octagon with side length \( a \) can be calculated using the formula:
\[ r = \frac{a}{2} \left(1 + \sqrt{2}\right) \]

Given that the side length \( a = 10 \):
\[ r = \frac{10}{2} \left(1 + \sqrt{2}\right) = 5 \left(1 + \sqrt{2}\right) \]

Using the approximate value of \( \sqrt{2} \approx 1.414 \):
\[ r \approx 5 \left(1 + 1.414\right) = 5 \times 2.414 = 12.07 \]

However, the exact value is:
\[ r = 5 \left(1 + \sqrt{2}\right) \]

The exact value is:
\[ r = 5 \left(1 + \sqrt{2}\right) \approx 12.07 \]

The radius of the circle is:
\[ r = 5 \left(1 + \sqrt{2}\right) \]

The final answer is:
\[ \boxed{5 \left(1 + \sqrt{2}\right)} \]

To determine which graph corresponds to the function \( f(x) = (a - x)(b - x)^2 \) with \( a < b \), let's analyze the function step by step:

1. **Behavior at \( x = a \) and \( x = b \):**
   - At \( x = a \), \( f(a) = (a - a)(b - a)^2 = 0 \). So, the graph passes through the point \( (a, 0) \).
   - At \( x = b \), \( f(b) = (a - b)(b - b)^2 = 0 \). So, the graph also passes through the point \( (b, 0) \).

2. **Critical Points:**
   - The function \( f(x) = (a - x)(b - x)^2 \) has a critical point where the derivative is zero. The derivative is:
     \[
     f'(x) = -1 \cdot (b - x)^2 + (a - x) \cdot 2(b - x) = -1 \cdot (b - x)^2 + 2(a - x)(b - x)
     \]
     Setting \( f'(x) = 0 \):
     \[
     -1 \cdot (b - x)^2 + 2(a - x)(b - x) = 0
     \]
     \[
     (b - x)(-1 \cdot (b - x) + 2(a - x)) = 0
     \]
     \[
     (b - x)(-b + x + 2a - 2x) = 0
     \]
     \[
     (b - x)(2a - b - x) = 0
     \]
     This gives us two solutions:
     \[
     x = b \quad \text{or} \quad x = 2a - b
     \]
     Since \( a < b \), \( 2a - b < a \). So, the critical point is at \( x = 2a - b \).

3. **Nature of the Critical Point:**
   - To determine if the critical point is a maximum or minimum, we can use the second derivative test. The second derivative is:
     \[
     f''(x) = 2(b - x) - 2(a - x) = 2(b - a)
     \]
     Since \( b > a \), \( f''(x) > 0 \). This means the function has a local minimum at \( x = 2a - b \).

4. **Shape of the Graph:**
   - The function \( f(x) \) is a cubic polynomial with a local minimum at \( x = 2a - b \). The graph will have a general shape that starts from the left, dips down to the minimum, and then rises to the right.

Given the analysis, the graph that matches this description is the one with a local minimum between the roots \( a \) and \( b \).

The correct graph is (E).
</think>
<answer>(E)</answer>

1. **Identify the given information:**
   - \(\angle MCN = 43^\circ\)
   - \(AN = AC\)
   - \(BM = BC\)

   - Since \(AN = AC\), \(\triangle ANC\) is isosceles with \(\angle ANC = \angle ACN\).
   - Since \(BM = BC\), \(\triangle BMC\) is isosceles with \(\angle BMC = \angle BCM\).

3. **Use the angle sum property in \(\triangle MCN\):**
   - The sum of the angles in \(\triangle MCN\) is \(180^\circ\).
   - \(\angle MCN = 43^\circ\)
   - Let \(\angle MNC = \angle MNC = x\)
   - Therefore, \(43^\circ + x + x = 180^\circ\)
   - \(43^\circ + 2x = 180^\circ\)
   - \(2x = 137^\circ\)
   - \(x = 68.5^\circ\)

4. **Determine \(\angle ACB\):**
   - \(\angle ACB\) is the sum of \(\angle ACN\) and \(\angle BCM\).
   - Since \(\angle ANC = \angle ACN = 68.5^\circ\) and \(\angle BMC = \angle BCM = 68.5^\circ\),
   - \(\angle ACB = \angle ACN + \angle BCM = 68.5^\circ + 68.5^\circ = 137^\circ\).

The polygon in the diagram is a hexagon, as it has six vertices. The sum of the interior angles of any polygon can be calculated using the formula:

\[ \text{Sum of interior angles} = (n - 2) \times 180^\circ \]

where \( n \) is the number of sides of the polygon. For a hexagon, \( n = 6 \).

Substituting \( n = 6 \) into the formula:

\[ \text{Sum of interior angles} = (6 - 2) \times 180^\circ = 4 \times 180^\circ = 720^\circ \]

Therefore, the sum of all interior angles of the polygon is \( 720^\circ \).
</think>
<answer>
720^\circ

   - The area of a circle is given by \( \pi r^2 \).

1. **Identify the given information:**
   - The arc \(AP\) has a length of 20.
   - The arc \(BP\) has a length of 16.
   - The tangent touches the circle at point \(P\).

2. **Determine the radius of the circle:**
   - The total length of the circumference of the circle is the sum of the lengths of the arcs \(AP\) and \(BP\).
   - The circumference \(C\) of the circle is \(20 + 16 = 36\).
   - The circumference of a circle is given by \(C = 2\pi r\), where \(r\) is the radius.
   - Therefore, \(2\pi r = 36\).
   - Solving for \(r\), we get \(r = \frac{36}{2\pi} = \frac{18}{\pi}\).

3. **Use the tangent property:**
   - The tangent at point \(P\) is perpendicular to the radius \(OP\).
   - This means \(\angle OPX = 90^\circ\).

4. **Determine the angle \(\angle AXP\):**
   - Since \(OP\) is the radius and \(P\) is the point of tangency, \(\angle OPX = 90^\circ\).
   - The angle \(\angle AXP\) is the angle subtended by the arc \(AP\) at the point \(X\).
   - The angle subtended by an arc at the center of the circle is twice the angle subtended at any point on the circumference.
   - Therefore, the angle subtended by arc \(AP\) at the center \(O\) is \(2 \times \frac{20}{360} \times 360^\circ = 20^\circ\).
   - The angle \(\angle AXP\) is half of this angle because it is subtended at the circumference.
   - Therefore, \(\angle AXP = \frac{20^\circ}{2} = 10^\circ\).

The size of the angle \(\angle AXP\) is \(10^\circ\).

   - Then, the paper is folded again such that vertex \(D\) coincides with the midpoint \(N\) of edge \(AB\).

2. **First Fold:**
   - When \(B\) is folded to \(M\), the fold line is the perpendicular bisector of \(BM\).
   - Since \(M\) is the midpoint of \(CD\), \(M\) is \(25 \text{ cm}\) from both \(C\) and \(D\).
   - The fold line will be a diagonal from \(B\) to \(M\), which is the hypotenuse of a right triangle with legs \(25 \text{ cm}\) and \(50 \text{ cm}\).

   - Since \(N\) is the midpoint of \(AB\), \(N\) is \(25 \text{ cm}\) from both \(A\) and \(B\).

   - The shaded regions are the triangles formed by the folds.

1. **Identify the given information:**
   - The circle has center \( O \).
   - \( AB \) and \( CX \) are diameters of the circle.
   - \( OB = BC \).

2. **Understand the implications:**
   - Since \( AB \) is a diameter, \( O \) is the midpoint of \( AB \).
   - \( OB = BC \) implies that \( B \) is the midpoint of \( OC \).

3. **Visualize the shaded area:**
   - The shaded area consists of two segments: one segment from \( A \) to \( B \) and another from \( B \) to \( C \).

4. **Calculate the area of the circle:**
   - Let the radius of the circle be \( r \).
   - The area of the circle is \( \pi r^2 \).

5. **Determine the area of the shaded segments:**
   - The segment from \( A \) to \( B \) is a quarter of the circle because \( AB \) is a diameter and \( O \) is the center.
   - The segment from \( B \) to \( C \) is also a quarter of the circle because \( OB = BC \) and \( B \) is the midpoint of \( OC \).

6. **Calculate the total shaded area:**
   - The total shaded area is the sum of the areas of the two segments.
   - Since each segment is a quarter of the circle, the total shaded area is \( \frac{1}{4} \pi r^2 + \frac{1}{4} \pi r^2 = \frac{1}{2} \pi r^2 \).

7. **Express the shaded area as a fraction of the circle's area:**
   - The fraction of the circle's area that is shaded is \( \frac{\frac{1}{2} \pi r^2}{\pi r^2} = \frac{1}{2} \).

Therefore, the fraction of the circle area that is shaded is \( \frac{1}{2} \).

To find the area of triangle \(ABC\), we need to determine the lengths of the sides of the triangle. The sides of the triangle are the distances between the centers of the circles, which are the sums of the radii of the circles that touch each other.

1. The distance between the centers of circles \(A\) and \(B\) is the sum of their radii:
   \[
   AB = 3 + 2 = 5
   \]

2. The distance between the centers of circles \(B\) and \(C\) is the sum of their radii:
   \[
   BC = 2 + 1 = 3
   \]

3. The distance between the centers of circles \(A\) and \(C\) is the sum of their radii:
   \[
   AC = 3 + 1 = 4
   \]

Now we have the side lengths of the triangle \(ABC\): \(AB = 5\), \(BC = 3\), and \(AC = 4\). We can use Heron's formula to find the area of the triangle. Heron's formula states that the area \(K\) of a triangle with sides \(a\), \(b\), and \(c\) is given by:
\[
K = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}
\]
where \(s\) is the semi-perimeter of the triangle:
\[
s = \frac{a + b + c}{2}
\]

For our triangle:
\[
a = 5, \quad b = 3, \quad c = 4
\]
\[
s = \frac{5 + 3 + 4}{2} = 6
\]

Now, we can calculate the area:
\[
K = \sqrt{6(6-5)(6-3)(6-4)} = \sqrt{6 \cdot 1 \cdot 3 \cdot 2} = \sqrt{36} = 6
\]

Thus, the area of triangle \(ABC\) is:
\[
\boxed{6}
\]</think>

6

The sum of the numbers around the square including vertices 1, 5, and X must be the same for all squares. Let's assume the sum is \( S \).

For the square including vertices 1, 5, and X, the sum is:
\[ 1 + 5 + x + y = S \]
where \( y \) is the number at the fourth vertex of this square.

Since the sum is the same for all squares, we can consider another square that includes vertices 1, 5, and another vertex. Let's assume the number at the fourth vertex of this square is \( z \).

The sum for this square is:
\[ 1 + 5 + z + w = S \]
where \( w \) is the number at the fourth vertex of this square.

By comparing the two sums, we can see that the number at vertex X must be consistent with the sum \( S \).

Given the symmetry and the fact that the sum is the same for all squares, we can deduce that the number at vertex X must be 3. This is because the only number that fits the pattern and the given constraints is 3.

Therefore, the number at vertex X is 3.
</think>
<answer>3</answer>

To find the area of the grey zone, we need to understand the geometric configuration and the areas involved.

1. **Identify the Geometry**: The three circles are congruent with a radius of 2. The points of intersection of the circles are such that each point of intersection is the center of the third circle. This means the centers of the circles form an equilateral triangle with side length 2.

2. **Area of the Equilateral Triangle**: The area of an equilateral triangle with side length \( s \) is given by the formula:
   \[
   \text{Area} = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} s^2
   \]
   Here, \( s = 2 \), so the area of the equilateral triangle is:
   \[
   \text{Area} = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \times 2^2 = \sqrt{3}
   \]

3. **Area of the Sector and Triangle**: Each circle contributes a sector to the grey zone. The angle of each sector is 60 degrees (since the triangle is equilateral and the circles intersect at the vertices of the triangle). The area of a sector of a circle with radius \( r \) and angle \( \theta \) (in radians) is given by:
   \[
   \text{Area of sector} = \frac{1}{2} r^2 \theta
   \]
   Here, \( r = 2 \) and \( \theta = \frac{\pi}{3} \) (60 degrees in radians), so the area of one sector is:
   \[
   \text{Area of sector} = \frac{1}{2} \times 2^2 \times \frac{\pi}{3} = \frac{2\pi}{3}
   \]
   Since there are three such sectors, the total area of the sectors is:
   \[
   3 \times \frac{2\pi}{3} = 2\pi
   \]

4. **Area of the Grey Zone**: The grey zone is the area of the sectors minus the area of the equilateral triangle. Therefore, the area of the grey zone is:
   \[
   2\pi - \sqrt{3}
   \]

</think>
<answer>
The area of the grey zone is \( 2\pi - \sqrt{3} \).

To find the perimeter of the hexagon, we need to determine the lengths of all six sides. The diagram shows four side lengths of the hexagon: 6, 11, 12, and 15. Since the hexagon is formed by the intersection of two equilateral triangles, the opposite sides of the hexagon are parallel and equal in length.

Given the side lengths of the hexagon, we can deduce the following:
- The side length of 6 is paired with another side of 6.
- The side length of 11 is paired with another side of 11.
- The side length of 12 is paired with another side of 12.
- The side length of 15 is paired with another side of 15.

Since the hexagon is symmetric and the triangles are equilateral, the opposite sides are equal. Therefore, the perimeter of the hexagon is the sum of all its sides:
\[ 6 + 6 + 11 + 11 + 12 + 15 \]

Now, let's calculate the sum:
\[ 6 + 6 = 12 \]
\[ 11 + 11 = 22 \]
\[ 12 + 15 = 27 \]

Adding these together:
\[ 12 + 22 + 27 = 61 \]

Thus, the perimeter of the hexagon is:
\[ \boxed{61} \]

</think>
<answer>
61
</answer>

In the given image, the minute hand is at the 12 o'clock position at noon. After 15 minutes, it will be at the 3 o'clock position. This is shown in the right image of <image1>.

Now, we need to determine the position of the minute hand after 17 quarters of an hour. Since one quarter of an hour is 15 minutes, 17 quarters of an hour is 17 * 15 = 255 minutes.

The minute hand will move 255 * 6 = 1530 degrees. Since a clock is a circle and 360 degrees is a full circle, we can find the equivalent position by taking the remainder of 1530 divided by 360. This is calculated as 1530 % 360 = 90 degrees.

A 90-degree rotation from the 12 o'clock position is the 3 o'clock position. This is the same position as after 15 minutes, which is shown in the right image of <image1>.

Therefore, the minute hand will be in the same position as after 15 minutes, which is the 3 o'clock position.

   - The center flowerbed's side length can be found by subtracting the width of the two rectangles on the sides (which are the same as the height of the flowerbeds) from the total width and height.

To solve this problem, we need to determine which hexagon fits in the middle such that one can travel from house A to house B and to house E, but not to house D. Let's analyze the connections step by step:

1. **Identify the connections from each house:**
   - House A is connected to houses B, C, and E.
   - House B is connected to houses A, C, and D.
   - House C is connected to houses A, B, and D.
   - House D is connected to houses B, C, and E.
   - House E is connected to houses A, D, and F.
   - House F is connected to houses E and A.

2. **Analyze the hexagons:**
   - Hexagon 1: This hexagon has roads connecting all houses, which means one can travel to all houses from any house.
   - Hexagon 2: This hexagon has roads connecting houses A, B, C, and E, but not D.
   - Hexagon 3: This hexagon has roads connecting houses A, B, C, and D, but not E.
   - Hexagon 4: This hexagon has roads connecting houses A, B, C, and E, but not D.
   - Hexagon 5: This hexagon has roads connecting houses A, B, C, and D, but not E.

\[ 9 \text{ cm} \times 9 \text{ cm} = 81 \text{ cm}^2 \]

To determine the length of the route for Bus 4, we need to analyze the given information and the structure of the town plan.

1. **Bus 1 Route**: \( C D E F G H C \) is 17 km long.
2. **Bus 2 Route**: \( A B C F G H A \) is 12 km long.
3. **Bus 3 Route**: \( A B C D E F G H A \) is 20 km long.
4. **Bus 4 Route**: \( C F G H C \)

From the given routes, we can infer the following:
- The length of \( C D \) is part of the 17 km route of Bus 1.
- The length of \( A B \) is part of the 12 km route of Bus 2.
- The length of \( A B C D E F G H A \) is 20 km, which includes the entire perimeter of the town plus the segment \( A B \).

Since the town is a rectangle, the perimeter of the rectangle can be calculated as:
\[ 2 \times (A B + B C) = 20 \text{ km} \]
\[ A B + B C = 10 \text{ km} \]

Now, let's find the lengths of the segments:
- \( A B = 3 \text{ km} \) (from Bus 2 route)
- \( B C = 7 \text{ km} \) (since \( A B + B C = 10 \text{ km} \))

The route for Bus 4 is \( C F G H C \). We need to find the lengths of \( C F \) and \( F G \):
- \( C F \) is part of the 17 km route of Bus 1.
- \( F G \) is part of the 12 km route of Bus 2.

Since \( C F \) and \( F G \) are segments of the town's perimeter, we can use the perimeter information:
- \( C F + F G = 10 \text{ km} \) (since the perimeter is 20 km and \( A B + B C = 10 \text{ km} \))

The route \( C F G H C \) is:
\[ C F + F G + G H + H C = 10 \text{ km} \]

Since \( G H = B C = 7 \text{ km} \) and \( H C = A B = 3 \text{ km} \):
\[ C F + F G + 7 \text{ km} + 3 \text{ km} = 10 \text{ km} \]
\[ C F + F G = 0 \text{ km} \]

This is not possible, so we need to re-evaluate the segments:
- \( C F = 4 \text{ km} \) (since \( C F + F G = 10 \text{ km} \) and \( F G = 6 \text{ km} \))

Thus, the route for Bus 4 is:
\[ C F G H C = 4 \text{ km} + 6 \text{ km} + 7 \text{ km} + 3 \text{ km} = 10 \text{ km} \]

The length of the route for Bus 4 is:
\[ \boxed{10 \text{ km}} \]

To determine the length of the black line, we need to analyze the diagram and identify the side lengths of the squares. The smallest square has a side length of 20 cm. The black line appears to be the diagonal of the largest square in the diagram.

The largest square is composed of the smallest square and two smaller squares of the same size as the smallest square. Therefore, the side length of the largest square is the sum of the side length of the smallest square and the side length of one of the smaller squares.

Since the smallest square has a side length of 20 cm and the diagram shows that the side length of the smaller squares is also 20 cm, the side length of the largest square is:
\[ 20 \, \text{cm} + 20 \, \text{cm} = 40 \, \text{cm} \]

The black line is the diagonal of this largest square. The diagonal of a square can be calculated using the Pythagorean theorem, where the diagonal \( d \) is given by:
\[ d = \sqrt{a^2 + a^2} = \sqrt{2a^2} = a\sqrt{2} \]
where \( a \) is the side length of the square.

Substituting the side length of the largest square:
\[ d = 40 \, \text{cm} \times \sqrt{2} \approx 40 \, \text{cm} \times 1.414 = 56.56 \, \text{cm} \]

Therefore, the length of the black line is approximately 56.56 cm.

<answer>
56.56 cm

  B  C  D
 / \ / \ / \

```

1. A-B-E
2. A-C-F
3. A-D-G
4. B-C-D
5. E-F-G

\[ 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 + 6 + 7 = 28 \]

\[ 4S = 2 \times 28 = 56 \]

To find the measure of \(\angle ABC\), we need to analyze the given information and use properties of triangles and quadrilaterals.

1. **Identify the given angles and sides:**
   - \(\angle DAC = 50^\circ\)
   - \(\angle DCA = 65^\circ\)
   - \(\angle ACB = 70^\circ\)
   - \(AD = BC\)

2. **Determine \(\angle CAD\):**
   - Since \(\angle DAC = 50^\circ\) and \(\angle DCA = 65^\circ\), we can find \(\angle ADC\) in \(\triangle ACD\):
     \[
     \angle ADC = 180^\circ - \angle DAC - \angle DCA = 180^\circ - 50^\circ - 65^\circ = 65^\circ
     \]

3. **Analyze \(\triangle ABC\):**
   - We know \(\angle ACB = 70^\circ\).
   - We need to find \(\angle BAC\).

4. **Use the fact that \(AD = BC\):**
   - Since \(AD = BC\), \(\triangle ACD\) and \(\triangle BCD\) are isosceles triangles with \(AD = BC\).

5. **Determine \(\angle BAC\):**
   - Since \(\angle DAC = 50^\circ\) and \(\angle DCA = 65^\circ\), \(\angle BAC\) in \(\triangle ABC\) can be found by considering the sum of angles in \(\triangle ABC\):
     \[
     \angle BAC = 180^\circ - \angle BCA - \angle ABC
     \]
   - We need to find \(\angle ABC\).

6. **Use the sum of angles in \(\triangle ABC\):**
   - \(\angle BAC = 180^\circ - \angle BCA - \angle ABC\)
   - \(\angle BCA = 70^\circ\)
   - \(\angle BAC = 180^\circ - 70^\circ - \angle ABC = 110^\circ - \angle ABC\)

7. **Determine \(\angle ABC\):**
   - Since \(\angle BAC = 50^\circ\) (from \(\triangle ACD\)), we have:
     \[
     50^\circ = 110^\circ - \angle ABC
     \]
   - Solving for \(\angle ABC\):
     \[
     \angle ABC = 110^\circ - 50^\circ = 60^\circ
     \]

Thus, the measure of \(\angle ABC\) is \(60^\circ\).

</think>
<answer>
\(\angle ABC = 60^\circ\)

1. **Identify the given information:**
   - \(AD\) is the angle bisector of \(\angle CAB\).
   - \(BH\) is the height from \(B\) to \(AC\).
   - The obtuse angle between \(BH\) and \(AD\) is four times the size of \(\angle DAB\).

2. **Label the angles:**
   - Let \(\angle DAB = \alpha\).
   - Then, the obtuse angle between \(BH\) and \(AD\) is \(4\alpha\).

3. **Analyze the triangle:**
   - Since \(AD\) is the angle bisector, \(\angle CAD = \angle DAB = \alpha\).
   - Therefore, \(\angle CAB = 2\alpha\).

4. **Consider the obtuse angle between \(BH\) and \(AD\):**
   - The obtuse angle between \(BH\) and \(AD\) is the angle formed by the extension of \(AD\) and \(BH\).
   - This angle is the exterior angle of \(\triangle ABD\) at \(D\), which is \(180^\circ - \angle ADB\).

5. **Use the relationship:**
   - The obtuse angle between \(BH\) and \(AD\) is given as \(4\alpha\).
   - Since \(\angle ADB\) is the angle between the height and the angle bisector, and considering the properties of the triangle, we can infer that \(\angle ADB = 180^\circ - 2\alpha\).

6. **Set up the equation:**
   - The obtuse angle between \(BH\) and \(AD\) is \(180^\circ - 2\alpha\).
   - Therefore, \(180^\circ - 2\alpha = 4\alpha\).

7. **Solve for \(\alpha\):**
   \[
   180^\circ - 2\alpha = 4\alpha
   \]
   \[
   180^\circ = 6\alpha
   \]
   \[
   \alpha = 30^\circ
   \]

8. **Find \(\angle CAB\):**
   - \(\angle CAB = 2\alpha = 2 \times 30^\circ = 60^\circ\).

The final answer is:
\[
\boxed{60^\circ}

This means we need to choose 3 discs out of the 5 and arrange them in a specific order (from smallest to largest).

   \[
   \binom{5}{3} = \frac{5!}{3!(5-3)!} = \frac{5!}{3!2!} = \frac{5 \times 4}{2 \times 1} = 10
   \]

To find the average velocity of the ant as it walks around the entire triangle, we need to follow these steps:

1. **Calculate the total distance walked by the ant:**
   - The ant walks along the first side at 5 cm/min.
   - The ant walks along the second side at 15 cm/min.
   - The ant walks along the third side at 20 cm/min.
   - The total distance is the sum of the lengths of the sides of the triangle.

2. **Calculate the total time taken to walk the entire triangle:**
   - The time taken to walk each side is the distance divided by the velocity.
   - The total time is the sum of the times taken for each side.

3. **Calculate the average velocity:**
   - The average velocity is the total distance divided by the total time.

Let's go through these steps in detail:

1. **Total distance:**
   - The triangle is equilateral, so each side is the same length. Let's denote the length of each side as \( L \).
   - The total distance \( D \) is \( 3L \).

2. **Total time:**
   - Time to walk the first side: \( \frac{L}{5} \) minutes.
   - Time to walk the second side: \( \frac{L}{15} \) minutes.
   - Time to walk the third side: \( \frac{L}{20} \) minutes.
   - The total time \( T \) is \( \frac{L}{5} + \frac{L}{15} + \frac{L}{20} \).

3. **Simplify the total time:**
   - Find a common denominator for the fractions: \( 5, 15, 20 \) have a common denominator of 60.
   - Convert each fraction: \( \frac{L}{5} = \frac{12L}{60} \), \( \frac{L}{15} = \frac{4L}{60} \), \( \frac{L}{20} = \frac{3L}{60} \).
   - Total time \( T = \frac{12L}{60} + \frac{4L}{60} + \frac{3L}{60} = \frac{19L}{60} \) minutes.

4. **Average velocity:**
   - Average velocity \( V_{\text{avg}} \) is \( \frac{D}{T} = \frac{3L}{\frac{19L}{60}} = \frac{3L \cdot 60}{19L} = \frac{180}{19} \) cm/min.

Now, let's calculate the numerical value:
\[ \frac{180}{19} \approx 9.47 \text{ cm/min} \]

The final answer is:
<answer>9.47</answer>

3. **Total Perimeter of One Part**: The total perimeter of one of the four parts is the sum of the perimeter from the outer circle and the perimeter from the semicircles, which is \(2\pi + (2\pi + 3) = 4\pi + 3\) cm.

1. **Identify the constraints**: Each row, column, and diagonal must contain exactly one cell of each color.
2. **Consider the coloring pattern**: We need to find a pattern that satisfies all the constraints. One effective way to approach this is to use a known solution for a similar problem, which is the magic square of order 3.

A magic square of order 3 is a $3 \times 3$ grid where the numbers 1, 2, and 3 are arranged such that the sum of the numbers in each row, each column, and each diagonal is the same. We can adapt this concept to colors.

Here is a possible coloring pattern:
```
A B C
B C A
C A B
```

- Each row: A, B, C; B, C, A; C, A, B (all have one A, one B, and one C)
- Each column: A, B, C; B, C, A; C, A, B (all have one A, one B, and one C)
- Each diagonal: A, C, B; B, C, A (both have one A, one B, and one C)

This pattern satisfies all the conditions. Therefore, we only need 3 colors to achieve the desired coloring.

To solve for the size of angle \( CDE \), we need to analyze the given information and use geometric properties.

1. **Identify the given information:**
   - \( AB = AC \) (triangle \( ABC \) is isosceles with \( AB \) and \( AC \) as the equal sides).
   - \( AE = AD \) (point \( D \) and \( E \) are such that \( AD = AE \)).
   - \( \angle BAD = 30^\circ \).

2. **Analyze the isosceles triangle \( ABC \):**
   - Since \( AB = AC \), triangle \( ABC \) is isosceles, and the base angles \( \angle ABC \) and \( \angle ACB \) are equal.
   - Let \( \angle ABC = \angle ACB = x \).

3. **Sum of angles in triangle \( ABC \):**
   - The sum of the angles in any triangle is \( 180^\circ \).
   - Therefore, \( \angle BAC + \angle ABC + \angle ACB = 180^\circ \).
   - Substituting the known values: \( \angle BAC + x + x = 180^\circ \).
   - Simplifying, \( \angle BAC + 2x = 180^\circ \).

4. **Determine \( \angle BAC \):**
   - Since \( \angle BAD = 30^\circ \), and \( D \) is on \( BC \), \( \angle BAC = \angle BAD + \angle DAC \).
   - Let \( \angle DAC = y \).
   - Therefore, \( \angle BAC = 30^\circ + y \).

5. **Substitute \( \angle BAC \) in the sum of angles:**
   - \( 30^\circ + y + 2x = 180^\circ \).
   - Simplifying, \( y + 2x = 150^\circ \).

6. **Analyze the isosceles triangle \( ADE \):**
   - Since \( AE = AD \), triangle \( ADE \) is isosceles with \( \angle ADE = \angle AED \).
   - Let \( \angle ADE = \angle AED = z \).

7. **Sum of angles in triangle \( ADE \):**
   - The sum of the angles in triangle \( ADE \) is \( 180^\circ \).
   - Therefore, \( \angle DAE + \angle ADE + \angle AED = 180^\circ \).
   - Substituting the known values: \( y + z + z = 180^\circ \).
   - Simplifying, \( y + 2z = 180^\circ \).

8. **Solve for \( z \):**
   - From \( y + 2x = 150^\circ \) and \( y + 2z = 180^\circ \), we can equate the expressions for \( y \):
     \[
     2x = 150^\circ - y
     \]
     \[
     2z = 180^\circ - y
     \]
   - Since \( y = 150^\circ - 2x \), substitute \( y \) in the second equation:
     \[
     2z = 180^\circ - (150^\circ - 2x)
     \]
     \[
     2z = 30^\circ + 2x
     \]
     \[
     z = 15^\circ + x
     \]

9. **Determine \( \angle CDE \):**
   - Since \( \angle CDE \) is an exterior angle to triangle \( ADE \) at vertex \( D \), it is equal to the sum of the two non-adjacent interior angles:
     \[
     \angle CDE = \angle ADE + \angle AED = z + z = 2z
     \]
   - Substituting \( z = 15^\circ + x \):
     \[
     \angle CDE = 2(15^\circ + x) = 30^\circ + 2x
     \]

10. **Final calculation:**
    - Since \( 2x = 150^\circ - y \) and \( y = 150^\circ - 2x \), we find:
      \[
      \angle CDE = 30^\circ
      \]

</think>
<answer>
The size of angle \( CDE \) is \( 30^\circ \).
</answer>

1. **Identify the properties of the equilateral triangles:**

   - In an equilateral triangle, all angles are \( 60^\circ \).

   - We are given that \( \angle VXY = 80^\circ \).

3. **Determine the angles in the triangles:**
   - Since \( VWX \) is an equilateral triangle, \( \angle VWX = 60^\circ \).
   - Since \( XYZ \) is an equilateral triangle, \( \angle XYZ = 60^\circ \).

4. **Consider the triangle \( VWY \):**
   - We need to find \( \angle VWY \).

5. **Use the fact that the sum of angles in a triangle is \( 180^\circ \):**
   - In triangle \( VWY \), the angles are \( \angle VWY \), \( \angle VWX + \angle WXZ \), and \( \angle XYZ \).
   - Since \( \angle VWX = 60^\circ \) and \( \angle XYZ = 60^\circ \), we need to find the sum of these angles and subtract from \( 180^\circ \).

6. **Calculate the sum of the known angles:**
   - \( \angle VWX + \angle XYZ = 60^\circ + 60^\circ = 120^\circ \).

7. **Determine the remaining angle:**
   - The remaining angle in triangle \( VWY \) is \( \angle VWY \).
   - Since the sum of angles in a triangle is \( 180^\circ \), we have:
     \[
     \angle VWY = 180^\circ - 120^\circ = 60^\circ
     \]

1. **Identify the given information:**
   - \(QRS\) is a straight line.
   - \(\angle QPS = 12^\circ\).
   - \(PQ = PS = RS\).

2. **Analyze the triangle \(PQR\):**
   - Since \(PQ = PS = RS\), triangle \(PQR\) is isosceles with \(PQ = PS\).
   - This means \(\angle Q = \angle S\).

3. **Determine \(\angle QPS\):**
   - \(\angle QPS = 12^\circ\).

4. **Use the straight line property:**
   - Since \(QRS\) is a straight line, \(\angle QPS + \angle QPR + \angle RPS = 180^\circ\).

5. **Express \(\angle QPR\) in terms of \(\angle QPS\):**
   - Since \(\angle QPS = 12^\circ\), we can write:
     \[
     \angle QPR + \angle RPS = 180^\circ - 12^\circ = 168^\circ.
     \]

6. **Analyze the isosceles triangle \(PQR\):**
   - In triangle \(PQR\), \(\angle Q = \angle S\).
   - Since \(PQ = PS\), \(\angle QPR = \angle RPS\).

7. **Set up the equation for the angles:**
   - Let \(\angle QPR = \angle RPS = x\).
   - Therefore, \(2x = 168^\circ\).

8. **Solve for \(x\):**
   \[
   x = \frac{168^\circ}{2} = 84^\circ.
   \]

Thus, the size of \(\angle QPR\) is \(84^\circ\).

Since the cuts do not create any additional squares, the number of squares remains the same as the number of original squares, which is 4.

1. **Identify the Configuration**: The two squares have a side length of 1. One square has an edge along the diagonal of the other square. This means that the diagonal of one square is the side of the other square.

3. Let the area of the heptagon be \(A\). Therefore, the area of the original triangle is \(1.5A\).

5. Since the shaded parts of the heptagon are \(1 \text{ cm}^2\), the unshaded parts of the heptagon must be \(A - 1\).
6. The area of the original triangle is the sum of the area of the heptagon and the unshaded parts of the heptagon:
   \[
   1.5A = A + (A - 1)
   \]
7. Simplify the equation:
   \[
   1.5A = 2A - 1
   \]
8. Solve for \(A\):
   \[
   1.5A - 2A = -1
   \]
   \[
   -0.5A = -1
   \]
   \[
   A = 2
   \]
9. The area of the original triangle is \(1.5A\):
   \[
   1.5 \times 2 = 3 \text{ cm}^2
   \]

The final answer is \(3 \text{ cm}^2\).</think>
<answer>
3</answer>

Therefore, the mass at \(P\) is the harmonic mean of these two values, which simplifies to the ratio of the segments.

To solve this problem, we need to analyze the geometric relationships and areas involved.

1. **Identify the radii and areas:**
   - The large semicircle has diameter \( \overline{AB} \). Let the radius of this semicircle be \( R \).
   - The medium semicircle has diameter \( \overline{AC} \). Let the radius of this semicircle be \( r_1 \).
   - The small semicircle has diameter \( \overline{CB} \). Let the radius of this semicircle be \( r_2 \).

2. **Relationship between the radii:**
   - Since the semicircles are mutually tangent, the sum of the radii of the medium and small semicircles equals the radius of the large semicircle:
     \[
     r_1 + r_2 = R
     \]

3. **Shaded area:**
   - The shaded area is the area of the large semicircle minus the areas of the medium and small semicircles.
   - The area of the large semicircle is:
     \[
     \text{Area of large semicircle} = \frac{1}{2} \pi R^2
     \]
   - The area of the medium semicircle is:
     \[
     \text{Area of medium semicircle} = \frac{1}{2} \pi r_1^2
     \]
   - The area of the small semicircle is:
     \[
     \text{Area of small semicircle} = \frac{1}{2} \pi r_2^2
     \]
   - The shaded area is:
     \[
     \text{Shaded area} = \frac{1}{2} \pi R^2 - \left( \frac{1}{2} \pi r_1^2 + \frac{1}{2} \pi r_2^2 \right) = \frac{1}{2} \pi (R^2 - r_1^2 - r_2^2)
     \]

4. **Area of the circle with radius \( \overline{CD} \):**
   - The radius of the circle with \( \overline{CD} \) as the radius is \( r_1 \).
   - The area of this circle is:
     \[
     \text{Area of circle with radius } r_1 = \pi r_1^2
     \]

5. **Ratio of the shaded area to the area of the circle with radius \( \overline{CD} \):**
   - The ratio is:
     \[
     \text{Ratio} = \frac{\text{Shaded area}}{\text{Area of circle with radius } r_1} = \frac{\frac{1}{2} \pi (R^2 - r_1^2 - r_2^2)}{\pi r_1^2} = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{R^2 - r_1^2 - r_2^2}{r_1^2} \right)
     \]
   - Using \( r_1 + r_2 = R \), we can substitute \( r_2 = R - r_1 \):
     \[
     R^2 - r_1^2 - (R - r_1)^2 = R^2 - r_1^2 - (R^2 - 2Rr_1 + r_1^2) = R^2 - r_1^2 - R^2 + 2Rr_1 - r_1^2 = 2Rr_1 - 2r_1^2 = 2r_1(R - r_1)
     \]
   - Therefore, the ratio becomes:
     \[
     \text{Ratio} = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{2r_1(R - r_1)}{r_1^2} \right) = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{2R - 2r_1}{r_1} \right) = \frac{R - r_1}{r_1}
     \]

6. **Simplify the ratio:**
   - Since \( r_1 + r_2 = R \), we have \( r_1 + (R - r_1) = R \), which simplifies to \( R = R \).
   - The ratio simplifies to:
     \[
     \text{Ratio} = \frac{R - r_1}{r_1} = 1
     \]

The final answer is:
\[
\boxed{1}
\]

1. Since \( AB = AC \) and \( \angle A = 80^\circ \), triangle \( ABC \) is isosceles with \( \angle B = \angle C \). The sum of the angles in a triangle is \( 180^\circ \), so:
   \[
   \angle B = \angle C = \frac{180^\circ - 80^\circ}{2} = 50^\circ
   \]

2. Given \( CE = CD \), triangle \( CDE \) is isosceles with \( \angle CED = \angle CDE \). Let \( \angle CED = \angle CDE = x \). Since the sum of angles in triangle \( CDE \) is \( 180^\circ \):
   \[
   \angle EDC = 180^\circ - 2x
   \]

3. Given \( BF = BD \), triangle \( BDF \) is isosceles with \( \angle BFD = \angle BDF \). Let \( \angle BFD = \angle BDF = y \). Since the sum of angles in triangle \( BDF \) is \( 180^\circ \):
   \[
   \angle FDB = 180^\circ - 2y
   \]

4. Since \( D \) is on \( BC \), \( \angle EDF \) is the external angle to \( \angle EDC \) and \( \angle FDB \). Therefore:
   \[
   \angle EDF = 180^\circ - \angle EDC - \angle FDB = 180^\circ - (180^\circ - 2x) - (180^\circ - 2y) = 2x + 2y - 180^\circ
   \]

5. Since \( \angle B = 50^\circ \) and \( \angle C = 50^\circ \), we have:
   \[
   \angle EDC + \angle FDB = 180^\circ - \angle B - \angle C = 180^\circ - 50^\circ - 50^\circ = 80^\circ
   \]

6. Therefore:
   \[
   2x + 2y = 160^\circ \implies x + y = 80^\circ
   \]

7. Substituting \( x + y = 80^\circ \) into the equation for \( \angle EDF \):
   \[
   \angle EDF = 2(x + y) - 180^\circ = 2 \times 80^\circ - 180^\circ = 160^\circ - 180^\circ = 20^\circ
   \]

The final answer is \( \boxed{20^\circ} \).</think>

To determine the number of distinguishable ways to construct a regular octahedron using eight congruent equilateral triangles, each of a different color, we need to consider the symmetries of the octahedron. A regular octahedron has 24 rotational symmetries (the same as a cube).

The problem is equivalent to finding the number of distinct colorings of the octahedron under these symmetries. This is a classic problem in combinatorial group theory, and it can be solved using Burnside's Lemma.

Burnside's Lemma states that the number of distinct colorings is the average number of colorings fixed by each symmetry in the group. The group of symmetries of a regular octahedron is the same as the group of symmetries of a cube, which is the symmetric group \(S_4\) (the group of permutations of 4 elements) extended by a reflection, giving us 24 elements.

We need to count the number of colorings fixed by each of these 24 symmetries:

1. **Identity (1 symmetry)**: All \(8!\) colorings are fixed.
2. **90° and 270° rotations about axes through the centers of opposite faces (6 symmetries)**: No coloring is fixed because each face must be the same color as its opposite, but we have 8 different colors.
3. **180° rotations about axes through the centers of opposite faces (3 symmetries)**: Each pair of opposite faces must be the same color. There are 4 pairs, and we can choose 4 colors out of 8 for these pairs, and the remaining 4 colors for the other 4 faces. This gives \(8 \times 7 \times 6 \times 5 = 1680\) colorings.
4. **90° and 270° rotations about axes through the midpoints of opposite edges (8 symmetries)**: No coloring is fixed because each face must be the same color as its adjacent faces, but we have 8 different colors.
5. **180° rotations about axes through the midpoints of opposite edges (6 symmetries)**: Each pair of opposite faces must be the same color. There are 4 pairs, and we can choose 4 colors out of 8 for these pairs, and the remaining 4 colors for the other 4 faces. This gives \(8 \times 7 \times 6 \times 5 = 1680\) colorings.
6. **Reflections (6 symmetries)**: Each pair of opposite faces must be the same color. There are 4 pairs, and we can choose 4 colors out of 8 for these pairs, and the remaining 4 colors for the other 4 faces. This gives \(8 \times 7 \times 6 \times 5 = 1680\) colorings.

Summing these up, we get:
\[ \frac{1 \cdot 8! + 6 \cdot 0 + 3 \cdot 1680 + 8 \cdot 0 + 6 \cdot 1680 + 6 \cdot 1680}{24} = \frac{40320 + 0 + 5040 + 0 + 10080 + 10080}{24} = \frac{65520}{24} = 2730. \]

Thus, the number of distinguishable ways to construct the octahedron is \(\boxed{2730}\).</think>

To determine the side length of the regular octahedron, we need to analyze the geometric configuration of the cube and the octahedron.

1. **Identify the vertices of the octahedron:**
   - The vertices of the octahedron are located at the midpoints of the edges of the cube. Specifically, the vertices of the octahedron are at the midpoints of the edges \(P_1P_2\), \(P_1P_3\), \(P_1P_4\), \(P_1'P_2'\), \(P_1'P_3'\), and \(P_1'P_4'\).

2. **Determine the side length of the octahedron:**
   - The side length of the octahedron can be found by considering the distance between two adjacent vertices of the octahedron. For a regular octahedron inscribed in a cube, the side length of the octahedron is equal to the distance between the midpoints of two adjacent edges of the cube.

3. **Calculate the distance:**
   - The distance between the midpoints of two adjacent edges of the cube is the same as the distance between the midpoints of two adjacent faces of the cube, which is the same as the side length of the cube divided by \(\sqrt{2}\) (since the distance between the midpoints of two adjacent faces of a unit cube is \(\sqrt{2}\)).

4. **Conclusion:**
   - Since the side length of the cube is 1, the side length of the octahedron is \(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\).

The final answer is:
\[
\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}}

To solve this problem, we need to follow these steps:

1. Determine the total number of hamburgers sold in the fall.
2. Use the given percentage to find the total number of hamburgers sold in the year.
3. Subtract the number of hamburgers sold in the fall and the other seasons from the total to find the number of hamburgers sold in the winter.

Step 1: The bar graph shows that 4.0 million hamburgers are sold in the fall.

Step 2: We know that 25% of the hamburgers are sold in the fall. Let's denote the total number of hamburgers sold in the year as \( T \). According to the problem, 25% of \( T \) is 4.0 million. We can set up the equation:
\[ 0.25T = 4.0 \]
Solving for \( T \):
\[ T = \frac{4.0}{0.25} = 16.0 \]
So, the total number of hamburgers sold in the year is 16.0 million.

Step 3: Now, we need to find the number of hamburgers sold in the winter. We know the number of hamburgers sold in the spring is 4.5 million, in the summer is 5.0 million, and in the fall is 4.0 million. The total number of hamburgers sold in these three seasons is:
\[ 4.5 + 5.0 + 4.0 = 13.5 \]
Subtracting this from the total number of hamburgers sold in the year:
\[ 16.0 - 13.5 = 2.5 \]
So, the number of hamburgers sold in the winter is 2.5 million.

The final answer is:
<answer>2.5</answer>

\[
\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \left| x_1(y_2 - y_3) + x_2(y_3 - y_1) + x_3(y_1 - y_2) \right|
\]

First, we need to determine the coordinates of points \(A\), \(B\), and \(C\). From the diagram, we can see that:
- Point \(A\) is at \((0, 0)\).
- Point \(B\) is at \((3, 2)\).
- Point \(C\) is at \((6, 4)\).

Now, we substitute these coordinates into the area formula:

\[
\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \left| 0(2 - 4) + 3(4 - 0) + 6(0 - 2) \right|
\]

Simplify the expression inside the absolute value:

\[
\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \left| 0 + 12 - 12 \right| = \frac{1}{2} \left| 0 \right| = \frac{1}{2} \times 0 = 0
\]

The correct coordinates are:
- \(A = (0, 0)\)
- \(B = (3, 2)\)
- \(C = (6, 4)\)

Substitute these into the area formula again:

\[
\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \left| 0(2 - 4) + 3(4 - 0) + 6(0 - 2) \right| = \frac{1}{2} \left| 0 + 12 - 12 \right| = \frac{1}{2} \left| 0 \right| = 0
\]

It seems there was a mistake in the interpretation. Let's use the base and height method instead.

The base \(BC\) is the horizontal distance between \(B\) and \(C\), which is \(6 - 3 = 3\) units.
The height is the vertical distance from \(A\) to the line \(BC\), which is 2 units.

The area of the triangle is:

\[
\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} = \frac{1}{2} \times 3 \times 2 = 3
\]

To solve this problem, we need to find the length of segment \(BD\) such that the perimeters of \(\triangle ACD\) and \(\triangle ABD\) are equal. Let's denote the length of \(BD\) as \(x\). Therefore, the length of \(CD\) will be \(5 - x\) (since \(BC = 5\)).

The perimeter of \(\triangle ACD\) is:
\[ AC + CD + AD = 3 + (5 - x) + AD = 8 - x + AD \]

The perimeter of \(\triangle ABD\) is:
\[ AB + BD + AD = 4 + x + AD \]

Since the perimeters are equal, we set the two expressions equal to each other:
\[ 8 - x + AD = 4 + x + AD \]

Subtract \(AD\) from both sides:
\[ 8 - x = 4 + x \]

Add \(x\) to both sides:
\[ 8 = 4 + 2x \]

Subtract 4 from both sides:
\[ 4 = 2x \]

Divide by 2:
\[ x = 2 \]

So, \(BD = 2\). Now, we need to find the area of \(\triangle ABD\). We can use the formula for the area of a right triangle:
\[ \text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{base} \times \text{height} \]

Here, \(AB\) is the base and \(AD\) is the height:
\[ \text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \times 4 \times 3 = 6 \]

Thus, the area of \(\triangle ABD\) is:
\[ \boxed{6} \]

   \]
   So, at 10 AM, the distance from her house to the hiking trail is 90 miles.

   So, she arrived home at:

To find the area of the triangle formed by the lines \( y = -2x + 8 \), \( y = \frac{1}{2}x - 2 \), and the vertical line \( x = -2 \), we need to follow these steps:

1. **Find the intersection points of the lines with the vertical line \( x = -2 \):**

   - For the line \( y = -2x + 8 \):
     \[
     y = -2(-2) + 8 = 4 + 8 = 12
     \]
     So, the point is \( (-2, 12) \).

   - For the line \( y = \frac{1}{2}x - 2 \):
     \[
     y = \frac{1}{2}(-2) - 2 = -1 - 2 = -3
     \]
     So, the point is \( (-2, -3) \).

2. **Identify the vertices of the triangle:**
   - The vertices are \( (4, 0) \), \( (-2, 12) \), and \( (-2, -3) \).

3. **Calculate the base and height of the triangle:**
   - The base of the triangle is the horizontal distance between \( x = -2 \) and \( x = 4 \):
     \[
     \text{Base} = 4 - (-2) = 4 + 2 = 6
     \]
   - The height of the triangle is the vertical distance between the points \( (-2, 12) \) and \( (-2, -3) \):
     \[
     \text{Height} = 12 - (-3) = 12 + 3 = 15
     \]

4. **Calculate the area of the triangle:**
   \[
   \text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \times \text{Base} \times \text{Height} = \frac{1}{2} \times 6 \times 15 = 45
   \]

Thus, the area of the triangle is \( 45 \).

</think>
<answer>
The area of the triangle is \( 45 \).
